ChatGPTOpen sidebarFundamental Physics Contains SpoilersGPT-4Can anyone give me a valid argument to support this claim?
I personally disagree with this statement. I think that this claim is outdated and that new evidence from quantum mechanics shows the exact opposite.

"Causation not needed in fundamental physics. Causation is scientifically obsolete. In 1913, Bertrand Russell published an article "On the Notion of Cause" in which he argued that causation was an obsolete concept. Russell pointed out that modern physics, including Newtonian mechanics and Maxwellian electromagnetic theory as well as Einsteinâs special and general theories of relativity, describe the natural world by means of mathematical functions and equations. The words âcauseâ and âeffectâ do not appear in any of the fundamental laws of nature, nor do any of the other verbs that express causal relations, like âpushâ, âpullâ, ârepelâ, âattractâ, âfractureâ, âmergeâ, and so on. The laws simply describe how certain quantities, like mass, energy, position, and velocity, change and do not change over time."

The Atlas of Reality. A Comprehensive Guide to Metaphysics, R. Koons & T. Pickavance, Wiley Blackwell, 2017., p. 578ChatGPTTo support the claim that causation is not needed in fundamental physics and is considered scientifically obsolete, one could argue the following:Descriptive vs. Prescriptive: Physics fundamentally describes how systems evolve over time given their initial conditions and the governing equations. This description doesn't require the assignment of cause and effect; it merely stipulates a relationship between events.Predictive Power: The equations of physics, such as SchrÃ¶dinger's equation in quantum mechanics, do not specify causes; they provide predictive power. If we can predict the state of a system at any time given its initial state, the concept of causation does not add to the predictive or descriptive capacity of the equations.Quantum Mechanics: In quantum mechanics, certain interpretations (like the Copenhagen interpretation) imply that events can occur without a clear cause, as in the case of radioactive decay, where an atom decays without any apparent cause, and the occurrence is entirely probabilistic.Counterfactual Definiteness: Quantum mechanics also challenges the idea of counterfactual definitenessâwherein the precise outcome of an event could be determined if we knew all the causes. The Heisenberg Uncertainty Principle states that certain pairs of physical properties cannot both be known to arbitrary precision; thus, the precise causation cannot always be determined.Block Universe View: The block universe theory of time, which is consistent with the theory of relativity, suggests that time is another dimension like space. In this four-dimensional spacetime, all events are fixed, and the flow of time is an illusion. This view undermines the traditional notion of cause and effect unfolding through time.Entanglement: Quantum entanglement suggests that particles can be correlated in such a way that the state of one (regardless of the distance between them) instantly influences the state of another. This phenomenon seems to defy any classical causal explanation and implies that not all relationships in quantum mechanics can be understood in terms of simple causality.These arguments, while not universally accepted, provide a framework for understanding why some philosophers and physicists might view causation as a less fundamental aspect in the description of physical laws.I'm thinking about how someone would actually program and train an AGI. On the one hand, if you believe humans are just complex 'programmed' machines with only the illusion of free will, simply training and designing an AGI to make decision may be the answer. Alternatively, the use of random number generation could be employed to introduce 'decision capacity'. I don't know, just some food for thought. I guess I'm wondering what it would like for Chat-GPT which just predicts the next word to develop true decision-making capabilities and agency.ChatGPTThe development of an AGI (Artificial General Intelligence) with true decision-making capabilities and agency is a complex challenge that extends beyond the current capabilities of AI systems like ChatGPT. Here's what it would entail:Learning and Adaptation: An AGI would need to learn from a vast array of experiences and adapt to new and unforeseen circumstances. This goes beyond current machine learning, which is often limited to specific domains.Understanding and Reasoning: AGI would require a level of understanding and reasoning that matches human cognitive abilities. It should be able to comprehend abstract concepts, perform common sense reasoning, and apply knowledge to different domains.Goal-Oriented Behavior: Decision-making in AGI would involve setting goals, planning, and executing actions to achieve those goals, considering long-term consequences. This is different from the next-word prediction model of ChatGPT, which is not goal-oriented but reactive.Agency and Motivation: For true agency, an AGI would need some form of intrinsic motivation or drives. This is a highly debated topic as it touches on consciousness and self-awareness, which we do not currently understand well, let alone reproduce in a machine.Ethical and Moral Decision-Making: An AGI with agency would need to make decisions that align with ethical principles. This involves not just following programmed rules but also making judgments in situations where there may be conflicting values or no clear answers.Stochastic Elements: Introducing randomness (e.g., through random number generation) can simulate variability in decision-making, but true agency would require the ability to choose based on values, goals, and understanding, not just randomness.Integration of Emotions: Human decision-making is often influenced by emotions. An AGI might also need to simulate or genuinely experience affective states to mirror human-like decisions.Autonomy: AGI would need a level of autonomy that current AI lacks. It would need to operate without human intervention, formulating its own tasks and learning from its interactions with the world.Developing such an AGI raises both technical challenges and ethical concerns. On the technical side, it would likely require breakthroughs in understanding intelligence, consciousness, and the brain. Ethically, there would be significant considerations about control, rights, and the impact on society.Currently, AI does not have desires, intentions, or consciousness; it operates within a framework defined by human programmers. To reach AGI with true agency, we would need a paradigm shift in how AI systems are conceptualized, designed, and implemented.That was from Melissa Mullen

If you ask me what keeps ChatGPT from providing AGI, I would answer with just one word: backtracking ð¤.

Backtracking is at the basis of classical AI. It is the ability to understand that the system has taken a wrong decision (recent LLM literature calls it self criticism, demonstrating a great ignorance about the history of AI ð¤­).

Notice that this capacity is used _before_ acting, actually reasoning about the posible consequences of the actions, _before_ taking them.

As someone correctly mentioned, indeed, LLMs decision making capabilities are the same (well, similar..) to those a caenorhabditis elegans uses for surviving. The worm doesn't _reason_ about the consequences of its actions. It just takes them accordingly to what it perceives. If the actions are wrong, the worm dies.

What distinguishes us from a caenorhabditis elegans is the ability to predict, within a given extent, "what is going to happen", influencing it through actions in a goal oriented manner. Notice that our goals change from time to time, so you cannot achieve this behavior through  just reinforcement learning approaches. Notice also that this form of reasoning, although more correct than pure reaction, inherently takes some time and, alone, could be dangerous if you have to survive to lions in the savanna ð. That's why it doesn't substitute at all reactive reasoning, but it joins it.

You can find a similar distinction in the (often overused) dual processing theory (consider reading the famous book "Thinking fast and slow" by the Nobel prize Kahneman ð).

Unfortunately, what is typical in classical AI, it's not at all common in "modern" AI. An artificial neural network, however enhanced with deep learning capabilities (transformers, convolutional layers, whatsoever) just "takes the action". If the action is wrong.. well.. to late ð¤

In conclusion, if we can somehow consider "alive" a LLM, just like a worm, we are far from making them "human". We should merge LLM reactive capabilities with higher order ones (through some neurosymbolic approach?) but we are, probably, searching thdm at the wrong place ðChatGPTRicardo de Benedictus brings up an insightful point about the limitations of current AI systems, such as LLMs, in achieving AGI, emphasizing the concept of backtracking.Backtracking is indeed a fundamental part of classical AI, where systems have the ability to recognize incorrect decisions and revise them before taking action. This contrasts with many contemporary AI models, which are typically designed to make decisions based on the data they are fed and the patterns they have learned, without an intrinsic mechanism to question their own decisions preemptively.De Benedictus notes that the difference between simple organisms like Caenorhabditis elegans and humans includes our capacity for prediction and goal-oriented action. Humans not only react to stimuli but also anticipate the consequences of actions and can adjust their behavior accordingly. This predictive, goal-oriented behavior is dynamic and adaptable, which is not easily replicated by reinforcement learning alone because our goals are complex and can change over time.He also mentions dual processing theory, a cognitive theory popularized by Daniel Kahneman, which suggests that we have two systems of thought: a fast, instinctive, and emotional system, and a slower, more deliberative, and logical system. Most current AI, including LLMs, operates in a way more akin to the fast, instinctive system, making decisions based on immediate data without the slower, contemplative processes that humans use.The challenge, as de Benedictus points out, is to integrate the reactive capabilities of current AI with higher-order cognitive abilities. This might involve a neurosymbolic approach that combines neural networks (good at pattern recognition and prediction) with symbolic AI (good at logical reasoning and knowledge representation).His conclusion is that while an LLM might exhibit some lifelike behaviors or responses, akin to simple organisms, it lacks the more complex cognitive processes that make humans unique. For AI to approach human-like intelligence, it needs not only to react but to understand the context of its actions, to plan, and to adjust its behavior based on a complex and changing set of goals and information.A summary of the main points of this conversationChatGPTArgument Against Causation in Physics: The conversation began with a discussion about causation in physics, where a statement was made that causation is not a fundamental concept in modern physics, as argued by Bertrand Russell in 1913. This view is supported by the fact that fundamental physics equations do not include causal language.AGI and Decision-Making: The conversation shifted to the topic of artificial general intelligence (AGI) and its development. It was noted that AGI might not require traditional human-like decision-making and could involve randomness to simulate free will.ChatGPT and Decision-Making: The discussion then addressed ChatGPT's capabilities, emphasizing that it does not merely predict the next word but operates through complex neural networks. It was pointed out that while current AI can simulate decision-making, it lacks the depth of biological neurons and hormonal influences.AGI's Potential: Various perspectives on AGI were shared, ranging from skepticism about its ability to replicate the human mind, to the idea that AGI could emerge from the interplay of many efficient algorithms optimizing and selecting the best 'thoughts' or outputs.Backtracking in AI: Finally, the concept of backtracking as a key feature missing from current AI models, including LLMs, was discussed. This feature involves the system's ability to recognize and correct wrong decisions before taking action, which is seen as essential for achieving AGI. The current AI systems are likened to simple organisms that cannot anticipate the consequences of their actions. For AI to be more human-like, it must integrate reactive capabilities with higher-order cognitive functions.I saw an interesting coincidence on my facebook feed:

While "Hamlet" by Shakespeare and "Ender's Game" by Orson Scott Card are distinct stories with unique settings and plots, both deal with themes of strategy, morality, manipulation, the burden of leadership, and the consequences of violence â albeit in very different contexts."Hamlet" is a tragedy set in the Kingdom of Denmark and explores themes of revenge, treachery, and moral corruption. ChatGPTThe fundamental attribution error is a concept in social psychology where people tend to (often incorrectly) attribute someone else's actions to their character or personality, while attributing their own actions to their environment or situation. This bias can indeed make individuals feel like they are victims of circumstance, with little control over their lives, while they perceive others as having more agency and control.When considering "Hamlet" and "Ender's Game" through this lens:In "Hamlet," the protagonist feels like a victim of the treacherous circumstances created by his uncle, Claudius. Hamlet's hesitation and inaction could be seen as a struggle with the circumstances of his environment and the expectations placed upon him due to his royal lineage.In "Ender's Game," Ender Wiggin is manipulated by the adults who control his environment. His actions are heavily influenced by the situations in which he is placed, yet those in power judge him based on those actions, seemingly ignoring the extreme pressures he faces.Both narratives can be interpreted to show how characters are judged by others based on their actions without fully understanding or acknowledging the situational pressures they are under. This reflects the fundamental attribution error in that external observers may not see or may discount the environmental and situational factors that influence behavior.The connection you've drawn highlights how both stories might serve to expose this cognitive bias, reminding readers or viewers that what appears to be a character flaw or virtue could actually be a response to the situation rather than an inherent trait.
